"},{"Title":"Sometimes you have to lose yourself before you can find anything.","Date":"19 August 2012","Score":"1 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" I didn't go to very many new releases in the Seventies, and those I did see were usually driven by media buzz. Many of them managed to push the envelope in some way that made them controversial for the era. There was Joe (nudity and casual drug use), Little Big Man (portrayal of gay characters), and Looking For Mr. Goodbar (promiscuous sex). You only needed to see Deliverance one time to be affected by it forever. No amount of time can pass that will allow you to forget it's most memorable scenes - Dueling Banjos, THAT scene with Ned Beatty, Drew's horrific drowning and the dislocated arm, and finally Ed's nightmare hand coming up out of the water of the newly formed lake.I just watched the film again and the passage of time has not diminished it's forceful impact. The picture has a unique ability to put you in a canoe right alongside it's principal players and force one to react to the events on screen as a participant. It sets up all sorts of moral dilemmas regarding a reaction to Lewis's (Burt Reynolds) shooting of the mountain man, the hiding of the body, and of course the forcible rape of Bobby (Beatty). The reactions of the four principals run the gamut of emotions, and it's an uncomfortable prospect to determine which of the adventurers one identifies with most.The way director Boorman works with nature also needs to be commended. The rapids scene in which the men capsize and fight for their life is extremely well staged and is executed flawlessly. On the matter of Drew's (Ronny Cox) death, the picture sets up a myriad of conflicting opinions on how he fell into the river, leading to a unique topic of discussion all by itself. Personally, I've come to consider that he simply blacked out over his participation in the hiding of the mountain man's body, although the idea of suicide crossed my mind as well. The suspicion of a gunshot was a variable to add another layer of complexity to the men's plight, all occurring in rapid succession to make their situation all the more bewildering. This whole sequence was very finely crafted.There's another aspect of the picture worth mentioning as well, that being the unlikely manner in which Ed (Jon Voight) becomes the leader of the survivors after Lewis's injury. Recall that earlier scene in which Ed has difficulty in bearing down on a deer with his bow and arrow. This sets up the later scene in which he must find the fortitude to shoot the mountain man before he himself is killed. When I first saw the picture back in the day, it immediately brought to mind a similar scene in Straw Dogs (another groundbreaking film for it's use of violence) in which Dustin Hoffman's character agonized over shooting a bird, then wiping the blood off his hands in an effort to cleanse himself of an awful deed. Of course, Hoffman goes on a rampage to close out that story to protect his wife and property. Similarly, though to a lesser degree, Voight's character calls upon his remaining wits and strength to save the remainder of his party.There's one thing though that I would have changed in the story to make the ending more powerful. In Ed's nightmare, Drew's hand comes up out of the water and Ed wakes up in a cold sweat. I would have preferred an ending in which that actually occurred, thereby adding one more element of ambiguity to the story whereby the potential for discovering Drew's body along with the two dead hillbillies is left an open question. Even so, there's still enough here to marvel at in terms of conflict and suspense. It's a film worth watching for all of it's discomfort. "},{"Title":"An excellent backwoods horror with an ecological edge.","Date":"5 August 2010","Score":"1 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" Outdoor fanatic Lewis Medlock (Burt Reynolds) convinces pals Ed (Jon Voight), Bobby (Ned Beatty) and Drew (Ronny Cox) to join him on a canoe trip down the Cahulawassee River, before the area is turned into one enormous lake by the construction of a dam. Their trip turns into a nightmare, however, when Ed and Bobby are assaulted by vicious hillbillies, and the friends are forced to kill to survive.Not only is John Boorman's Deliverance one of the finest examples of back-woods survival horror ever made, it is also an excellent cautionary tale for anyone planning to exploit the great outdoors. Screw with the environment, the film warns, and the environment will screw you right back (most likely in the ass, and without the aid of lube).Deliverance's four suburban adventurers symbolise civilisation itself, and are content to use and abuse the wilderness to their own advantage while they can, but fail to understand the importance and potential danger of the terrain through which they are travelling. Although Lewis and his pals aren't particularly destructive themselves, they do display a lack of concern about the terrible fate awaiting the Cahulawassee River, and show little respect for the area and its inhabitants, bragging about 'beating' the river, and mocking the locals for their simple ways.Conversely, the river and the indigenous folk that inhabit the surrounding hills are representative of nature as a whole, and both have a hand in dishing out savage retribution to those outsiders who dare to mess them about.Unflinching, brutal, tense and exciting, Deliverance is a masterful exercise in storytelling from director Boorman, who achieves a gritty realism through his genuinely untamed locations, bravura performances from the film's incredible leads (who clearly canoe the river's rapids themselves), and the unconventional decision to cast 'locals' as secondary characters. The film also features a couple of iconic moments that have become synonymous with the whole murderous hillbilly backwoods sub genre the brilliant scene that sees Drew and an inbred youth participating in a musical duel armed with guitar and banjo respectively; and the harrowing rape of poor Bobby by a hillbilly ('Squeal like a pig, boy!'.... Weeeee..... weeeeee!).In fact, my only real criticism of the entire film is a touch of ropey day-for-night photography during Jon Voight's climb up a perilous cliff-face, but this minor technical imperfection ain't going to stop me from awarding maximum marks. Deliverance is one of the finest films from a radical period of film-making. That is, I think it is one of the best who done-it plots of all time. The fact that we never really know whether or not the four city slickers were being pursued from the start or whether or not Voight shot the wrong guy or not... But there are clues. Like the shot of three guys in the woods when the trip set out up river, when there were only two guys that followed them in the truck. At first we think it is the same guys that agreed to drive the cars. But where did the third come from? Were they even the same guys or some others? And why did the sheriff ask Voight if the two guys who agreed to drive the cars had a third with them? Did he suspect something about three crazed buggers haunting those parts? Or did he suspect that the missing brother-in-law may have been with them? And other minor clues... The guy that Voight shot on the top of the gorge had a wedding ring that the directors were key to focus on. Brother-in-law of the guy in Aintry as he suggested? Also, there aren't many bridges in that neck of the woods. The rape scene was on the right bank. Voight shot the second victim on the left bank. Careless or meaningless direction? I don't think so. Not from Boorman. But do I know what it all means? Hell no, but it is worth buying the DVD and doing a closer study. I think there is an answer in there somewhere. But if anyone else has it figured out, please let me know. Fascinating. "},{"Title":"Crimes against nature","Date":"12 October 2006","Score":"1 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" What is the underlying theme of this film? I think it is all about crimes against nature, as well as a think-piece concerning the dichotomy of man's uneasy coexistence with nature. Destroying a picturesque river setting by flooding it to create a man-made dam; spearing fish with a modified high-power bow/arrow; using the river's naturally-formed rapids for a week-end adventure with little appreciation for the natural surroundings or the people who live there, etc. Let us not fail to add forced homosexual rape by sodomy; killing others with impunity; man's attempted ability to manipulate reality by telling mutually-agreed false stories - vs- nature's ability to reveal only facts. There are so many more such references that can be similarly drawn. I think that is the theme James Dickey was working with. Combine the effective picturesque prose of Dickey's novel with Boorman's vividly beautiful filming of the story and the result is a memorable film; probably more appreciated by history than it was when the film was initially released. I would agree the acting is fine, yet may be the weakest part of the final product. Nonetheless, this is a fine film - one of my favorites. "}{"Title":"Stands up over time","Date":"12 October 2002","Score":"1 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":"  I just watched this movie again after 20 years and found it to be one of the most effective pieces of film of the last half-century.  This is a truly effective voyeur film, a film in which there are actually 5 people traveling down the river, the 4 characters and the viewer.  Notice that all scenes involve the four characters, you are never away from them.  John Boorman, who has given us such great films as Beyond Rangoon and The Emerald Forest provides us with a story of multiple layers and messages, most importantly is As you sow, so shall you reap in which those who rape the wilderness (the four adventurers and their way of life) are themselves raped and find the price of such rape is high indeed, in lives, in guilt. Boorman's direction is highly effective, using the voyeur technique with a high degree of dexterity, perhaps the best it has ever been used.  A case in point is the lack of music in many parts.  Since Psycho it has become tradition to use music to lead the audience into feeling a certain way. Boorman, however, leaves the music behind and allows the viewer to decide how he/she should feel.  Note amount of vocal silence?  It allows the viewer to contemplate what has transpired so far. He also sets up the villains nicely by giving a stereotypical concept a breath of life by introducing us to the inbred, backwoods folk, but never letting us get to know them or their way of life.  He uses several effective visuals at the beginning of the film (especially the old lady watching over the deformed girl) and the Dueling Banjos scene typifies the people of the land, simple, none-too-bright, and living in a world separate from our own.  As to acting, this is probably Jon Voight's best job, and Reynolds is held well in check. On a scale of 1-10, I rate this a good solid 9. It is a turbulent, disturbing story in which man stands at the crossroads of civilization and nature in all their respective speciousness and wildness.  Man by his very nature hominizes his world, but he can never totally control his environment.  Symbolism abounds in this allegorical tale, and it's my guess that the rednecks were an extension of the outside world `striking back' at modernity.  An outstanding work. "},{"Title":"Pulp Fiction in the Appalachian Rapids--Burt Reynolds Best Performance","Date":"25 March 2012","Score":"2 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" If some of the elements of 1994's Pulp Fiction were set in the Appalachians, you might get some idea about Deliverance. By the same director as Excalibur and Emerald Forest, Deliverance concerns four city-slicker types who decide to take a canoe trip down a river which is going to be flooded in order to pave the way for the construction of a dam. The city-slickers believe it will be the last time they will be able to experience the river as nature intended. They foresee the trip as simply a weekend of white water rafting, fishing, camping, and becoming one with their natural surroundings. Their experience eventually becomes an ordeal of survival and moral dilemma.Their first stop by car before they head to the river foreshadow some of the events which will follow. To get gas and ask for assistance, our four travelers stop at a village (I hesitate to call it even a town) made of shacks and broken-down cars. They get gas and ask the locals to drive their cars to the end of the river so they won't have to walk back the many miles from their departure point. Drew (Ronnie Cox), the one musician among the group, engages a youngster in a famous scene in which they play Dueling Banjos, which brought back this memorable instrumental blue-grass riff originally written in the 1950's. (It topped the charts as a result of the film.) After the piece is finished, Drew attempts to shake hands with the youngster who seems disturbingly aloof and almost not quite aware of the strangers in his midst. We then notice the other locals acting funny toward them. They are even warned not to go the river. Of course, we know right off they should have listened.The vacationers finally make it to the river and begin their journey. At first they engage in what seems nothing more than a scenic ride along the rapids with Lewis (Burt Reynolds) using his modern bow and arrow to snag game fish. They set up camp at night. But the next day, their journey escalates into something far more sinister and life-threatening, involving sexual assault and murder. The story twists into an ordeal in which we learn how each character deals with the dramatic, traumatic and tragic circumstances which become their experience. The journey is a flight into an entirely new world of existence.What makes Deliverance compelling is not simply the circumstances faced by the characters, but how the characters in turn deal with their situation. Each actor is right on the money, living in the skin of their characters, including Jon Voigt as Ed who seems ill-at-ease with hunting and rafting, Ned Beatty as Bobby nicknamed Chubby as the salesman who seems like a fish out of water, Ronnie Cox as Drew the musician but the most unstable of the group, and particularly Burt Reynolds as Lewis, the unofficial leader of the group who seems the most at-home. Later, Lewis is forced to relinquish his decision-making power and must put his life into the hands of his less-experienced colleagues.A film which is not for all tastes but makes you think, much in the way Pulp Fiction does. After the premier of Pulp Fiction, Tarantino said that not all audiences necessarily liked it, but they came away discussing it. I think Delieverance has much of the same impact. While this is not escapist entertainment, it is a film which will challenge your perceptions about survival versus law. Overall not to be missed if you're up for a challenge. But if your favorite movie is the Sound of Music or Lassie, you may want to pass on it. "},{"Title":"The vanishing wilderness","Date":"5 October 2011","Score":"2 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" The 1960s and 70s saw a lot more wild, outdoor pictures, partly because of improvements to cinematography and more mobile equipment opened up greater possibilities and lessened the need for studio shooting, but also partly because the hippy era resulted in a lot of movies with a getting-back-to-nature vibe. But this was also an age of questioning and uncertainty, and the wilderness could also be turned into a place of terror and mystery. This mixture of yearning and fear provides the backdrop for 1972 thriller Deliverance.Deliverance was the breakthrough movie for English director John Boorman. Boorman's earliest pictures had been very overtly stylised, with lots of zooms and rapid editing. For Deliverance he has a more subtle approach, but still with that strong sense of rhythm and atmosphere. Of especial note in Deliverance is his use of space on the river. Every shot shows a tight bend or is part obscured by the trees &#x96; we never really get a look at the way ahead. This is not an Aguirre/Apocalypse Now vision of the river as a road wending its way through the forest. This is the river as a maze where every part looks the same and there doesn't seem to be any way out. This greatly helps to fuel the sense of confusion and paranoia that permeates the whole thing. But Boorman further remembers to keep this a human drama, staging a lot of the action in close-up with the background an unfocused blur.This is all the better to capture some fine acting. Burt Reynolds is, at first glance, simply a macho poser, albeit a very charismatic one. But there is more going on in his performance, with hints of disturbing capabilities just below the surface. Ned Beatty impresses in his first screen role, allowing himself to be totally humiliated and still appearing believable in his reactions. On the downside Ronny Cox is hammy and Jon Voigt is a little overwrought. Towards the end there's a neat cameo by source novel author James Hickey, giving an understated but menacing turn as the local sheriff.Like so many pictures of this era, Deliverance states its themes boldly. There is the conflict between progress and rural simplicity, and the need for men to prove themselves in an age where technology has made everything easy and comfortable. Such statements seem a little trite, but in the case of Deliverance they are forgivable because they are presented in such a taut, compelling thriller, with layers of ambiguity that are cunning yet never convoluted, and certainly never pretentious. It is neither as grandiose or other-worldly as Heart of Darkness or its many adaptations (including Apocalypse Now), but is instead close, gritty and uncomfortably lifelike, even when at its most nightmarish. And Deliverance is about this fight, in three rounds, ending with a split decision.The first round is won by men, those politicians and businessmen who decided to terminate a natural setting for improving the lives of some bourgeois citizens. A beautiful river condemned to become a common lake, for no other specific reason than to assess the almighty power of men on the face of the Earth. This project scandalizes Lewis, Burt Reynolds, who takes his friends to a last canoe ride, to celebrate the swan song of a natural setting. A setting raped by men, but that these men will try to honor with respect, like three civilized men. But this is nature, not civilization, and nature is about survival and doesn't give a damn about respect, honor, or dignity. And the natural setting will become their hellish nightmare, and if men rape nature symbolically, unfortunately, nature doesn't give a damn about symbols &#x85;Lewis, is the one who enthusiastically embraces the call of the wild, like a fish in water, and the same can't be said for Bobby, Drew and Ed, Ned Beatty, Ronny Cox and Jon Voight as the straight men of a tragicomedy where nature has the good laughs and where Bobby will pay the most humiliating price. These men are so normal, so indeed civilized, that we do feel they will have to face a natural adversary incarnated by this wild river. And the iconic beauty of that film is that nature will be incarnated in its most savage and bestial way, through a population that lived in a world so closed, that it inspired the lowest instincts, starting by the most blasphemous one incest.It's not surprising that the movie indirectly features incest in its first scene through the unforgettable hybrid hillbilly who plays one of the most memorable cinematic instrumental duets ever &#x85; The 'Duelling Banjos' still resonate in my mind as the hymn announcing a coming battle of men against the elements, against God, nature, give it a name &#x85; but the scene is so cheerful, so full of joy and energy that you forget its most important aspect a warning, a warning about a population where common laws are not respected; after all, isn't the incest, the cardinal sin on which the whole human civilization was based? The first setting is obviously made of doors that open the gates to hell, a hell where there's no God, no rules, except the one of the strongest.And this is how the D of Deliverance became the epitome of cinematic Disturbingness, because it depicted one of the most difficult scenes to watch but still extremely eloquent about the &#x85; ways, bestiality choose to assert its power. The way these hillbillies acted had nothing to do with sexuality, it's a rape yes, but more a disgusting affirmation of power that puts men at the same level of animals, even lower. Animals prove their power by the same act, the hillbilly did, and it's not a coincidence that he wanted him to imitate the sound of an animal during that moment. Nothing sexual, no fantasies, living like an animal is acting like one, and the only way to get rid of this scum, was to ruthlessly kill him. Natural selection, an eye for an eye. At the end of the second round, it's a tie.And to understand the beauty of civilization is to understand its weakness. This rape is not gratuitous, it's a powerful and meaningful scene that illustrates our whole fears, as civilized men meeting nature in its cruelest and less empathetic form. Deliverance warns about the death of nature, but also the death of humanity. Both are in danger, and both help each other. Men can still respect nature and win over the elements, and nature can inspire men some instincts that save them in extreme situations. Not a pessimistic film, and what a thriller! I knew the film was considered to be a classic by many, but I was interested to see whether it held up at all in the light of modern cinema.It does, and then some.This is astonishing, ahead-of-its-time film-making and quite possibly the first man vs. nature movie. Since then, our screens have been deluged with endless backpackers/hikers/teenagers on a camping trip get lost in the woods with psycho rednecks for company-type movies, but DELIVERANCE is, as far as I know, the first and best of this cycle (although there are many other low budget gems, like the 1977 film RITUALS).The movie begins deceptively ordinary, with a group of guys going on a canoeing weekend and bantering together. Not a great deal happens until the infamous, still shocking sequence, which is all about power and degradation and still leaves a deeply unpleasant impact on the unassuming viewer. From this second in, the tension is riveted up and the film becomes chilling, disturbing, and to my mind, utterly frightening. As director, Boorman is at the peak of his game, shooting his rugged locales with an expertly eerie eye and making excellent decisions during the storytelling (I loved the uncertainty surrounding the fate of a central character).Four well-known actors were picked for the film and boy, do they give fine turns. Burt Reynolds is the typical stock macho guy he's well known for playing, but he gives him warmth and a likability that shines through despite his hard-ass, egotistical behaviour. Ned Beatty starts out as a rather unlikeable chap, but our opinion of him changes once he undergoes a terrible ordeal and he becomes the most sympathetic in the film. Ronny Cox (best known as the bad guy from ROBOCOP) is the straight, out of his element guy, and Jon Voight is the film's hero &#x96; a man who must delve inside himself to rediscover his primal instincts.The film is superbly shot throughout, the sense of menace is overwhelming, the plot is thoroughly engaging and the twists and turns always surprising. The ending is full of a different type of suspense and doesn't disappoint either, while Voight gives what I consider to be his best turn as the ordinary guy who becomes a hero. A quite wonderful movie. The story sports a certain poetic flavour with the exploration of themes such as the glory of nature and man's attempted conquest of it.The plot concerns a group of four friends who embark upon a canoeing trip down the Cahulawassee River in the Georgia backwoods. While the wild river poses a certain degree of danger these men soon find that the locals are more dangerous by far.The cast is a pretty good one with the best performance coming from the versatile Jon Voight. Burt Reynolds is a good fit as the leader of the group while Ned Beatty & Ronny Cox make convincing everymen. Special note goes to the actors playing the various mountain men since they manage to give the film an air of authenticity.John Boorman's direction is well-handled and was rewarded with an Oscar nomination. The film looks attractive throughout and also features some memorable music, most notably Duelling Banjos.Ultimately, I consider the film a well-executed thriller with plenty of depth. The notoriety of the film's single most famous scene may lead you to believe that it is otherwise unremarkable but that's not the case at all. "},{"Title":"Very well acted and written","Date":"30 April 2006","Score":"2 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" Of all the potentially disturbing films that I have seen, Deliverance upset me the most. I managed to rationalize the horror of other films -- after Jaws I reasoned that sharks could endanger people only if they enter the water and only at certain ocean beaches; and after Saving Private Ryan I decided that WWII ended long ago and that the armed forces didn't recruit people my age anyway -- but I couldn't think myself through the depression I experienced for 2-3 days following the night I watched Deliverance.The poverty of the people living above the Cahulawassee River, the hostility of some of them towards outsiders, their overall social and emotional limitations, as underscored by the banjo playing boy's autistic-like detachment as he looked down at the canoeists from a foot bridge, had me unnerved before the real adventure began. Looking back, I not only owed my fear to a well written story, but also to a wisely chosen and ably directed cast.Burt Reynolds did a very believable job in his portrayal of Lewis Medlock, the lone physical, backwoods smart, outdoors man of the foursome. With his self-assertive personality and confrontational style, Medlock seemed to know that a show of vulnerability does not gain respect from a mountain man. He aroused my anxiety in the process, however, because I couldn't imagine myself approaching those people in the same way.I saw myself much more in the other three men, Bobby Trippe, Drew Ballinger (Ned Beatty and Ronnie Cox), and especially Ed Gentry, Jon Voight's character. In fact, I identified so much with them, as they dealt with their fear by attempting to ingratiate themselves with the utterly unsophisticated people they encountered in the Appalachian wilderness, that I felt personally very threatened when Ballinger's, Trippe's, and Gentry's patronizing behavior seemed to work against them. "},{"Title":"Paradise Lost","Date":"4 February 2006","Score":"2 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" Before this movie was released, backwoods America was usually depicted by Hollywood as innocence incarnated filled with uneducated yet hardworking, moralistic, God-fearing people with common sense solutions to everyday problems. They only became violent to protect their families or to have a friendly fisticuffs with a rowdy full of moonshine. Deliverance showed rural America differently, rather than mean streets there were mean dirt roads and cold-blooded killers. Deformities brought on my poor diets and such taboos as incest practiced for generations led to a perversion of the entire community. In reality neither stereotype is correct. But that's the way the big screen depicted it. What caused the change? Possibly the permissive sixties and the national trauma of the Vietnam War. The four main characters of Deliverance each has a story to tell. There's the amoral adventurer who likes to kill for sport giving Burt Reynolds has best screen role. Reynolds makes the most of it and does a magnificent job. Too bad dry rot set in almost immediately with Reynolds making some very bad career decisions. Though he made a lot of money in such popular films as Smokey and the Bandit, his artistic potential was squandered. Then there's the sensitive poet musician played by Ronny Cox. His world is composed of sweetness and light. There's no room for imperfections, certainly not for evil. Ned Beatty shows his amazing screen talent for the first time as the obnoxious good ol' boy who's more at home in a cocktail bar than in the primeval forest. Jon Voight, who had a few years earlier been nominated for an Academy Award for Midnight Cowboy, plays the most complex character of the four. He's a wannabe but what does he want to be? He seems to emulate Lewis (Burt Reynolds) yet at the same time not wanting to be like him. This ambivalent attitude is further twisted by his hesitant attempt to kill, first a deer then a man. He also tends to be the actual leader of the men rather than Lewis, who seeks that position but doesn't have the leadership qualities necessary. All four men journey into the wilderness to canoe down the Cahulawassee River believing that this last vestige of the frontier before the dam waters cover it is a personal paradise far from the madding crowd of the big city. Soon the trip turns into a nightmare. The paradise becomes at best purgatory.The music adds so much to the film yet in reality it's just one song--although Drew (Ronny cox) does sing an old folk song in camp the first night. Dueling Banjos, originally Feuding Banjos, has just the right tempo and pitch to highlight the events of the movie. It starts off slowly, builds to a fast dance-type melody, then ends at a feverish lope, so fast the music seems to run away with itself. A bluegrass revival (if that's the right word since bluegrass was never a mainstream art form) was taking place in the United States due to the popularity of such shows as The Beverly Hillbillies and Bonnie and Clyde, both featuring the fabulous bluegrass team of Lester Flatt and Earl Scruggs. Virtuoso banjo picker Scruggs had almost single handedly revolutionized banjo playing with his own tuning methods. He had also written several songs especially for the instrument. Eric Weissberg took an old banjo piece from 1955 called Feuding Banjos recorded by Don Reno and Arthur Guitar Boogie Smith (later changed to Dueling Banjos by the Dillards)and played it for Lonnie (Billy Redden) and Drew (Ronny Cox) in the film. Today it is possibly better known than Scruggs' Foggy Mountain Breakdown.The first half of Deliverance seems to crest when the brutal sadistic rape scene occurs. Following that horrendous part, the film slows down somewhat and becomes a bit disjointed. There are a few memorable images during the second half, the confrontation with the hunter on the mountain top, the dining interaction when Bobby (Ned Beatty) breaks the ice by declaring, this corn is special, the car pulling over so the church could pass, moving the graves, and the terror of the hand, but this is still the least satisfying piece of the movie. Director John Boorman had arguably already done his best work with Point Blank, a still under-appreciated cinematic creation. But Deliverance is certainly one of his triumphs. James Dickey did a commendable job adapting his book to film. His acting adds a bit of authenticity to the goings on, unlike Ken Kesey who refused to have anything to do with the screen version of One Flew Over The Cuckoo's Nest.Though not without its flaws, this is one of my favorite flicks from the 1970's. In its own way it helps define that rather confused decade. "},{"Title":"A Potent Portrait Of Man In Extremis. Highly Recommended.","Date":"1 February 2006","Score":"2 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" A potent film. Four characters are developed, subtly and succinctly, and then, just as deftly, pushed over the edge. They set out to experience the wilderness of the Cahulawassee River and find it both wonderful and dangerously wild.Patient cinematography captures the river's beauty, isolation, and, later, its ominous force. Crisp, unhurried, often stationary shots let the Cahulawassee's rapids speak for themselves, but equally let the threat of the river's silent stretches resonate.The burbling, roaring, quiet water is this film's soundtrack. This lack, by and large, of extra-diagetic music perfectly suits the material; the audience never gets to experience everyday movieland. The one exception to this rule is the 'Duelling Banjos' theme, which, while initially forming part of the story, later echoes down the river to great effect.The song, as first exchanged between one of the canoers and an autistic hillbilly, is a deservedly famous showstopper. However, this film is far more than a song.The thrill and terror of exploration; the disappearance of wilderness; the light and dark sides of both nature and man; loyalty; faith in others and in society; democracy; the morality of killing; the clash of urban and rural culture; prejudice; notions of justice; the capacities of man in extremis; the consequences of secrecy; man 'after the fall' &#150; this film touches upon many complex and powerful themes, yet this richness is rarely conspicuous and never belaboured.Performances are universally excellent. John Voight's understated showing hypnotises looking into to his eyes we experience the film's trials, and on the other side we feel changed as he feels changed. Burt Reynolds and Ned Beatty are also convincing in their more exuberant roles. The secondary characters that populate Deliverance strikingly transport the film to the edge of civilisation. The lingering peep-show shots of Lewis's search for a driver are a disturbing portent.Highly Recommended. (Contains spoilers).","Date":"9 November 2003","Score":"2 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" This sumptuous looking film tells the story of four big city boys who go on a rafting adventure in the Deep South to find themselves but end up discovering things they rather wouldn't have. Echoing the great American novel Huck Finn they attempt to tame a treacherous stretch of the Mississippi before it is dammed by constructors and the naturalist theme of man not knowing or respecting his environment enough which rings through that book is seen here too, sometimes disturbingly. From the moment Burt Reynolds crashes a jeep recklessly through the woods trying to find the launch point by instinct having thrown away their map, one can tell that these men have no idea what awaits them. Reynolds is (for once) excellent as the archetypal alpha-male who leads his timid friends into the breach but is crippled by the river. Better still is Voight though, who finds it in himself to take another man's life; clearly the thinker amongst the group, his transition to cold blooded killer is utterly convincing to watch; the point, that we all still have our natural instincts within us but they are suppressed by our sanitized everyday lives, is well made. This transition is speeded by seeing his friend raped by bandits - again signifying how unnatural the whole episode is and how out of (their) place these boys from the city really are. The relief when they reach the end of their trip and return to civilization is palpable and heightened by how inevitably slow the descent into disaster has been. Indeed the whole film is wonderfully paced. Forget the much parodied banjo scene, this film is a disturbing and well written tale of civilized men going back to nature and discovering that they are neither ready nor willing for its trials. "},{"Title":"It's so subtle, it's often dismissed","Date":"25 July 2000","Score":"2 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":"  This film is actually quite subtle, despite its graphic violence, and so it is often dismissed too readily.  From its very beginning opening credit with the offscreen callow conversations against the majestic nature, all the way to the last shot of the river (and our minds imagining what may or may not emerge), this film contains intricacies few can equal.  When I first saw it as a sixteen year old, I thought Burt Reynolds's character had all the right answers, because of his masculinity and because he was Burt Reynolds. "},{"Title":"A masterful film that exposes a naked reality hiding behind male bravado.","Date":"13 September 1999","Score":"2 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":"  Deliverance is a movie that demands that its characters put up or shut up. I am sure this film has meant many things to many different people, but to me, this is one of the strongest themes of the movie.  Four city-slickers go into the wild for a weekend canoe trip, acting like trail-blazing pioneers courageously exploring a vanishing frontier.  But early in the story, we see evidence that they are way out of their element.  They have entered a place that they can't easily understand or come to terms with, without ever even leaving their own state.  As they look for someone to drive them and their canoes upriver, we see in the background the harsh poverty and backwardness of the mountain folk.  It is as if the four men are in a completely different world where all things new have failed to penetrate and the rules are not the same as in the big city.  Yet, they seem oblivious to it all, and go about their male-bonding by recklessly canoeing down a wild and untame river and drinking large amounts of beer as if they are in some Old Milwaukee commercial.  They have come to conquer the wilderness, much like civilization is infringing upon this forgotten place by damming up the river and flooding the gorge.  But as the trip suddenly goes horribly wrong, the men are forced to reveal what they are really all about inside.  Ned Beatty's frail character is stripped of all his dignity by a mountain man in a very disturbing yet powerful rape scene, and then has to rise off the ground and deal with his shame in it's aftermath.  Burt Reynold's character, a gungho survivalist freak is exposed as actually being weak when he is injured and has to rely on his friends to survive.  Ronny Cox seems doomed from the start as he fails to deal with the situation they all find themselves in.  And The character played by Jon Voight is faced with the morally trying task of killing a man to defend himself when earlier in the film he can't even bring himself to shoot a deer.  The film takes these four macho men and puts them to the test.  Just like in real life, some of the men pass the test, and some of them fail, but what they are really made of on the inside has by the end of the movie replaced what they have tried to say about themselves on the outside.  Machismo is humbled.  I particularly enjoy seeing this because in today's world, so many people, both men and women take unnecessary risks with their lives in order to achieve some ridiculously short-lived thrill.  Watching Voight's torturing ordeal to make it through this mess he has gotten himself into so he can get back alive to those he loves brings to light what is really important, and that there are plenty of thrills to be had making love to your wife and watching your children grow up and give you grandchildren. "},{"Title":"Action adventure film with apocalyptic sub-message","Date":"2 March 1999","Score":"2 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":"  Deliverance is a deceptively simple film. It can be enjoyed as a simple action adventure with four city dwellers battling for their lives when their weekend trip to the country goes very, very wrong.I suspect, however, that director Boorman was trying to tell us that we live in a jungle and man's inherent nature is evil.The four go on a canoeing trip and it is obvious that their only concerns are about natural perils but we see that the real danger comes both from themselves and other humans.Boorman makes stunning use of symbolic imagery when an extremely ugly hillbilly child plays with Cox in the two banjos sequence. Despite the beauty of the music we sense foreboding in what is to come.The crucial scene comes after Burt Reynolds kills one of the hillbillies to defend his colleagues from homosexual assault. The four debate whether to go to the authorities or cover up the killing. We see a courtroom-like atmosphere develop where what seems to be on trial is the nature of mankind itself. The personalities of each of the group are thrown into sharp contrast Ronny Cox is the humanist - he argues that the group should turn itself in and claim self-defense, firm in the belief that by doing the 'right thing' they will be treated fairly. Ned Beatty's character has tried throughout the film to prove his masculinity - his only concern is that no one should ever know of this episode (which could call his sexuality into question). Jon Voight is the vacillator - he will go with the majority or the strongest voice. He is therefore convinced by the character of Burt Reynolds. Reynolds is the strong silent type with no illusions about what to expect from a jury composed of the relatives of the dead man.In contrast to all the others Reynolds appears completely relaxed and is in his element. In reality, he has waited all his life for this moment. The seriousness with which he has taken this trip and the fact that he learned how to shoot with bow and arrow are no accident. Even before the killing, one never had the impression that the trip was just fun for him. One almost has the feeling that he is a closet survivalist. He has the self-confidence of a jungle native on his own turf and knows at every step from then on in the film what to expect and what to do.Predictably, there is a need for more killing before the survivors can escape. Voight is the hapless 'coward' put in the role of enduring severe physical and emotional ordeals to save the group and himself.When finally, all seems to be over we can not fail to ignore the irony of the 'advice' given by the sheriff (interestingly it appears that this was played by the author of the book and scriptwriter of the film James Dickey) don't ever do anything like this again = I know what you did but I can not prove it. You will not have the same luck if you try again.The group understand exactly what he means. They have learned all they want to about themselves and human nature in general. "},{"Title":"Deliverance Review","Date":"10 December 2015","Score":"","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" Deliverance is a timeless piece that captures the thrill of an action movie with the intensity of a thriller. The basis for the plot line consists of four friends who decide travel down the rapids of the Cahulawassee River before its floodplain is turned into a recreational lake. After passing off their vehicles to a few hillbillies, who promise to deliver them downstream, the company of canoers embark. But the hills that bank the river have eyes, and the intensity of the action that follows this realization is only surpassed by the connection made between the viewer and the film's unexpected protagonist. While most thrillers and action movies in the current era of film seem to feed off of high paced and unrealistic action sequences, Deliverance reels in the viewer through its realism. Its unedited feel and quality acting allow the viewer to empathize with each character as they deal with obstacles as real humans, as opposed to Hollywood heroes, would. Noteworthy is the liberal use of extreme close ups following significant events, as they become a significant means by which relationship is established. It is the ability of each actor to perform during these shots that sets the characterization in Deliverance apart from many other films. The only weakness in the film is Burt Reynolds' character, Lewis, whose overdone macho-man persona doesn't fit with the realistic characters the film so- successfully establishes. This factor is a significant hindrance to the film's goal of overall realism. Still, the value of the film is more noticeable now than ever, as it is a wonderful contrast to what seems to be the norm in the action genre. I would recommend this film to anyone of age who appreciates quality acting and a realistic plot. The moral implications raised by each character's actions also leave significant food for thought. "},{"Title":"Backwoods Bonding","Date":"19 November 2014","Score":"","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" This thriller concerns the exploits of four good old Southern boys trekking down a huge, thrashing river. Set in a very incestuous, back-hoe section of Georgia, the film becomes unsettling as soon as they venture into a small town in search for drivers. The infamous dueling banjo/guitar scene occurs right away, putting it in the forefront of your mind. In this perturbing setting the characters move around easily, stretching out their legs and hamming it up with one another. Though it's masked by the sexual assault and nature vs. man narrative the real theme of the film is the state of manliness and its cousin, machismo. Personified beefcake Burt Reynolds, provides the perspective of pure manliness in the character of Lewis he hunts and fishes with a crossbow, sleeps under a ramshackle homemade tent, sloughs through mansplaining monologues about his intrepid wanderings in the wilderness, and revels in his absurd testosterone fueled opinions on manhood. The other three are there to watch him boast and bray, themselves trying to find their manliness amongst the rushing rapids of the river. The story concerns the four men, but it's really the story of Ed (Voigt), who fails to live up to Lewis' expectations he can't hunt, can't protect himself and Bobby (Beatty), doesn't argue with Louis, and can't save Drew (Cox) from the river's wrath. Near the end of the film he is tasked with defending them and this time he succeeds, to his detriment. Everything serves as a metaphor in this film, including, unfortunately, the infamous piggy scene. Beatty is the only one of the four to eschew traditional roles of masculinity, and so he is the one who is victimized. Near the end of the film we think that this film has been about hillbillies threatening four men on the river, and when the second murder occurs it seems obvious that everything has been righted for our heroes. In fact, it serves as another metaphor, which shows that relying on animalism for decision-making incurs violent, and deadly, repercussions. The ending of the film dragged far too long, as this message is waylaid in order for guilt to be shifted between the remaining members of the troupe, belying the point of the rest of the film. Otherwise this was a thoughtfully crafted film about the role of manhood and how it denigrates men, making them murderous brutes. "},{"Title":"Squeal like a pig, etc, etc.","Date":"11 August 2014","Score":"","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" Deliverance is one of those movies that's defined by one scene (you probably know the one) in a way that really doesn't do justice to the film as a whole. Its central thematic concern is the changing relationship between the urban American and the wilderness. Deliverance smartly makes its protagonists Southern urbanites to mitigate the kind of cultural biases that a story about murderous hillbillies calls up, although it's still not exactly a positive portrayal of the Appalachians community. As much as our quartet of hikers profess a love of nature, their relationship with the interior is profoundly antagonistic, a mixture of antagonism and a desire to conquest. Their interaction with the locals is a synecdoche of the larger geological transformation hanging over the whole film the destruction of a complex wilderness to meet the water needs of growing cities.What's so refreshing about Deliverance is that it flatters absolutely no one. Burt Reynolds plays a primitivist alpha male, a role he would spend the next couple decades misinterpreting, and while his steely nihilism is attractive at first he is ultimately rendered useless. Ned Beatty and Ronny Cox are buffoons of entirely different stripes. Jon Voight plays a civilized man whose descent into savagery is never made heroic. Despite being famous for a scene of violence, Deliverance never revels in its depravity in the same way that some of its contemporaries do. These characters have some surprisingly philosophical debates, debates that are ultimately rendered meaningless as they scramble for pure survival. And as for the much-vaunted interior, it's represented by a pair of rapacious monsters who are just as intent on reducing people to animals as the city-dwellers are.John Boorman is a competent director but has trouble livening up the scenes that take place outside of the main action. The opening dueling banjos scene is brilliant, and basically sums up the entire film, but the initial rafting and camping is a slog and the epilogue stretches on too long. The lack of strong direction stops Deliverance from really being among the elite of the New Hollywood films, but it's a respectable entry into that tradition nevertheless. In terms of both its politics and its art, Deliverance captures a moment in time that was already vanishing, and does so in vivid and unforgettable fashion. The fictionally soon-to-be-no-more Cahulawassee river, which was really the Chattooga river, is still intact as a scenic wilderness area, and will remain so for a very long time into the future. Although a powerful drama from one perspective, Deliverance is a paranoid concoction of fear mongering and disinformation about the realities of the American wilderness and the people who populate it. The location was really chosen well. Indeed, this would still be a very nice film to watch, had the canoe trip gone smoothly. The lush forests and gentle landscape only make the horror more horrible. It has been said that Burt Reynolds' performance as the outdoorsman, Lewis, is the star acting role in this film. I reckon however, that Jon Voight steals the show with his role as the suburban family man, Ed, who is rapidly forced to change his demeanour in order to survive. In fact, the scene in which climbs the cliff was not a stuntman. It was Voight himself. To cut costs the filming wasn't insured and the actors did their own stunts. The soundtrack is particularly noteworthy. Eric Weissberg's and Steve Mendel's performance on guitar and banjo as part of the Duelling Banjos sequence remains one of the most awesome pieces of soundtrack in the history of cinema for the sheer intensity of its performance. It doesn't build suspense very well and it isn't really as gruesome as we have been led to believe. The plot itself is somewhat poor and it doesn't really go anywhere. Nevertheless, this movie has enough good points to get my recommendation. I did like it but for fans of gore, there isn't really much of it. None really, in fact. It isn't so much a horror film as an adventure film that turns a little bit sour. Think of it like Rambo First Blood meets Three Men In A Boat. Look out for a very young Charley Boorman as Ed's son. I did like this movie, the soundtrack, cinematography and acting earns it a well deserved 7 out of 10. The first half-hour or so of the film is peaceful and pastoral in character, with the scenery of the area lovingly photographed, and the friends spend their time hunting, fishing and shooting the white-water rapids on the river. Then the tone of the film abruptly changes when two of the adventurers meet two unfriendly locals. They are threatened at gunpoint, tied up and one of them is subjected to a homosexual rape. Just when it seems that they are about to be killed, they are saved by the arrival of their two companions, who kill one of the mountain men with a bow and arrow and drive off the other.What had started off as an adventure now turns into a nightmare. The four have to try and escape, terrified that the surviving mountain man will return seeking revenge. The river, once their playground, now seems like their prison. The rapids mean that they cannot return upstream, so the only way they can get out is by climbing the steep cliffs of the gorge through which the river passes or by pressing onwards though even more dangerous rapids. (A more recent thriller, The River Wild, has exploited a similar situation).Tensions arise within the group as they quarrel about how best to proceed and whether to report the man's death to the authorities.This was one of a number of films that were made possible by the relaxation in the sixties and seventies of the cinema's strict code of morality but which seemed deeply shocking. What caused the controversy here was not so much the killing (there is less bloodshed than in the average Western, and certainly less than in the average war film) as the scene of the homosexual assault. Until the sixties any direct mention of homosexuality was virtually taboo, and a scene like this one would have been unthinkable. Many of those films seemed ground-breaking then but look very dated today, generally those which were deliberately made as erotica (like the Emmanuelle series) or for their shock value (like Caligula). Some directors, however, including John Boorman, used the freedom given by the liberalisation of censorship restrictions for serious purposes.In some respects Deliverance is similar to another controversial film made the previous year, Sam Peckinpah's Straw Dogs. In that film too there is a clash between urban and rural lifestyles, a confrontation which turns violent as the city-dwellers defend themselves against hostile rustics, and a controversial rape scene. Both Peckinpah and Boorman intended their films to make a statement about violence, and both looked at the subject not from the viewpoint of the original perpetrators but from the viewpoint of victims who themselves resort to violence in retaliation or self-defence. Deliverance, however, is in my view the better film. Whereas the battle between Dustin Hoffman and his attackers in Straw Dogs always seems implausible, Boorman makes the four men's predicament seem frighteningly real.Another reason why Deliverance succeeds is the excellent acting of the four principals, especially Burt Reynolds and Jon Voight. This is important because the four main characters are all individualised. Reynolds's character Lewis, self-assured and confident, starts off as the leader of the group, but can be complacent and self-satisfied, underestimating the potential dangers of their situation. He is strongly against to informing the police about the killing, and in this he is opposed by the more liberal Drew who believes in sticking to the letter of the law. Bobby, the victim of the rape, is traumatised by his experience. Voight's character, Ed, is initially reserved and timid, but emerges as the leader after Lewis is injured. At first he is either too compassionate or too timid to kill a deer, but later finds himself capable of killing a man. (Reynolds's performance came as a revelation to me, as previously I had simply regarded him as the mustachioed embodiment of seventies machismo).On one level, Deliverance is an exciting and very tense thriller (and a very good one as well). There is, however, more to it than that. The film is also about character, a study in leadership, of the changing dynamics within the small group, and of how men react to danger, whether that danger comes from others or from the forces of nature. It touches on themes of the male psyche, of violence in society and of man's relationship to nature. A very good film indeed. 8/10 "},{"Title":"Four men decide to canoe down a river to prove their manhood.","Date":"12 November 2005","Score":"","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" John Boorman's Deliverance is one of the best films of the 1970s. Deliverance contains something simplistic that never fails to shock, excite and horrify altogether. Yet even since 1972 it is a testament to the golden oldies that never diminish from the audience's mind. It is very fast paced and aggressive from the start, something you can watch in an hour and a half, yet still the naturalistic approach of director John Boorman remains true to the original story; it is not over sensationalised with music or flimsy acting, or under achieved in any way. It is an account of the terrors that lurk in the backwater, small country towns where strangers are neglected, threatened, terrorised. Much of the movies' story balances on the horrific male rape scene; perpetuated with slow burning use of editing that builds up the tension to danger levels. It's a modern classic; the year of Clockwork Orange; ultra violence at it's best when it seems to come true. Yet still, Deliverance remains even after the land is gone beneath the raging waters. It's one thing to perform dangerous stunts for the camera with insurance--extremely risky business without, as in this case.  Of course, no reputable company would risk financing a cast and crew in paddleboats braving the treacherous rapids of South Carolina's lake jocassee and Georgia's Chattooga River.  Especially commendable was Mr. Voight's volunteering to do his own stuntwork, in one scene scaling a 95 degree mountainside--with Vilmos Zsigmond's camera teetering close at hand.  Yet it is these potentially perilous scenes which give this drama its special edge.Fortunately for all concerned, no serious physical mishaps occurred, and the production wrapped intact.  There's no doubt that all involved realized they were working on a very high quality project, and elected to give it their all.  Mr. Reynolds in particular may chalk this one up as his finest career achievement. Whatever concerns may have occurred between Director Boorman and Writer Dickey, along with other productional challenges, is now moot, as appreciative audiences continue to be thrilled by this solid enactment of Dickey's novel-based screenplay. "},{"Title":"Maybe not a shocker by today's standards ...","Date":"7 August 2000","Score":"","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":"  I've just been reading all of the comments about this movie and was interested by the different points of view of those submitting them. I have to say, first of all, that I totally agree with the person who said that you SHOULD ALSO READ THE NOVEL. Like any other story, all of the various elements within it can NEVER be transferred to the screen - novels are just too long and involved. I totally DISAGREE that Boorman did a crappy job of directing or that this film in ANY WAY stinks. The first time that I saw this is when it came out in 1972. In judging this picture, you need to remember that societal expectations were a LOT different (in America) than they are today. EVERYONE was talking about the rape scene in this movie. NO ONE had filmed something like that, that I know of - or that I remember anyone else ever having heard of being in a movie at that time. Most probably, the issue hadn't been addressed within a novel, either - until Dickie wrote this. I was 14 when I snuck in to see this - and it had an impact even though I knew what was coming. I'd read the book right after seeing it - and, again, it's MUCH more involved and dramatic. A few years ago, I took a course called The Male In American Literature and was surprised and pleased to find that Deliverance was on the syllabus as one of the stories we'd be studying. In re-reading this as an adult (and after a higher education) there were many things I had never noticed before. Dickie uses a LOT OF foreshadowing in the story - right down to something that no one else in the class noticed, but I did the color orange is often associated with caution and he mentions it a whole lot of times. I went back and watched the movie again, as well - it had been many years since I had first seen it. I did notice what another reviewer mentions here - that you never do see the identity of that other person in the tow truck - and I mentioned to my friend that for the first time I thought it was those same guys who were involved in accosting the canoeing party further downriver. Someone else mentions here (in these reviews) about how the kid who had played Dueling Banjoes with Drew doesn't even acknowledge him when they are passing under him, under the bridge. Again - foreshadowing - another subtle hint that something terrible is about to happen. I don't think that this story - whether the book or the novel - is a bad one at all. I agree totally with the person who said that there are much deeper elements at work here. If you thought the movie stunk, read the book - then go back and give the movie another chance. You'll understand the story better and will see it in a totally different light. It is also interesting how in this story, like in Harper Lee's American classic To Kill A Mockingbird, the author raises the question if killing another human being is ever justified. I don't think that any of can really say, with complete conviction and assuredness, that we never could. I sure as hell wouldn't have wanted to go through something like that canoe trip. But we all have our own baggage in life, don't we? All have our own stories to tell - if not about ourselves and things that have happened to us, then certainly about horrible things which have happened to someone we know. In closing, I'd just like to say that whoever compared this to Blair Witch ...  Well, let's just say that I think any 5-year-old could have made the latter. There's NO comparison in literary value, so to speak. I debated asking for my money back from renting the tape. Had I spent the extra money to see it at the theater, I definitely would have walked out half way through, and would most likely have demanded my money be returned. Deliverance is NOT trash. The characters are VERY realistic, the story line is haunting in subtle undertones, and the acting is nothing short of EXCELLENT. Personally, I can't see how anyone would think any different of it, but we're all entitled to our opinion. Four men who wanted to go on a innocent canoe trip before the stream became home to industrialism, endure the worst weekend of their lives.First of all, I've gotta comment on Burt Reynolds as the Alpha male. He was perfect for this role - his manner, his dress, his style. Ronny Cox seemed to be the one who strategized his horrors by having the law outside of where they were take care of the problems. Jon Voight proved to be more than the fair haired boy, the family man who had to reach deep in his soul to make decisions and survive, and Ned Beatty was obviously the weakest and got the worst of the ordeal - on the trip and after.Second there is John Boorman, the director. Everything he directs has a backdrop of nature, environment and humans interactions in it. This piece is no different - but it's something you wouldn't know unless you watch other John Boorman films, and you should. Then go back and watch this one uncut. He has a knack for exposing the deepest darkest roots of nature and man's fascination with it.Third, there are the quotable phrases I heard for years and had no idea they came from this movie. You gotta real pretty mouth and Squeal like a pig are in the top 10 of quotable lines. And music? "},{"Title":"When We're Free To Roam","Date":"13 July 2006","Score":"","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" Deliverance is mixture of drama and horror. The film manages to show how a group of ordinary men who are just taking are short break can get wound up in such meaningless acts of terror. The first half hour of Deliverance is like watching a different film to the one that comes out latter. The first scenes show these normal men just having a good time and some beautiful imagery of the American land that surrounds them. The scenes after this are very different, the film all of a sudden turns out to be very dark and asks the viewer What would would you do in this situation? This is disturbing and shows just how when people are not around the cops become cruel and meaningless. This all leads up to showing how human nature can be so evil when left to it's bitter beginning.The film is definitely not one of entertainment value and is a film a film to be studied and thought about long after the chilling and depressing closing credits. What makes the film so depressing is that the action that causes everything to kick off is meaningless and so immoral that it is heart wrenching. You also feel as though the film is teasing you with the purposely inappropriate banjo music as the soundtrack. Deliverance is very different to most films, it's not afraid to put the viewer through such tense and nail-biting situations. Not only is the film disturbing, it is also very sad. Every single one of these men's lives are automatically altered through such a terrifying experience. "},{"Title":"Masterpiece of Suspense","Date":"8 February 2009","Score":"2 out of 4 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" Deliverance (1972)**** (out of 4)Jon Voight, Burt Reynolds, Ned Beatty and Ronny Cox plays friends from the city who decide to go to the country so that they can white water before the river is turned into a lake. The trip starts off fine but soon the four are working against the odds to get off the river with their lives after they encounter a couple rednecks. To me this is one of the greatest films of the decade but I think it's also one of the most chilling ever made and still packs a wonderful punch after all these years. To me the movie is about nature and trying to survive in it no matter what gets thrown at you. Out of the four characters only one, Reynolds, claims to know what he's doing yet as the men get closer into darkness the other find themselves trying to fight through their own fears. The movie has one great sequence after another and so many memorable moments including the terrific Dueling Banjos sequence, which of course eventually leads to the nightmarish sequence in the woods that everyone knows about now. The pig sequence has become someone of a joke now due to everything that's been on television but to me this scene still packs one hell of a punch because of how terrifying it is. The performances by Bill McKinney and Herbert Coward are downright chilling and really add to the terrifying situation these men are faced with. The nightmarish tone, almost like a horror film, doesn't end here because the rest of their trip down the river will have the viewer holding their breath because of the unknown, which is going to follow. I always thought the river played a big part in the film because it seems when the water is calm so are the men. It gives them a chance to breathe but when the rapids pick up so does the dangerous situations going through them. The calmness before the rape leads to the men leaving in a rush, confused and not knowing exactly what's to follow and then they run into the faster rapids again. The calmness then brings them to the next situation of the mountain, which is a brilliantly executed sequence. Another major plus is some wonderful cinematography, which to me is among the best ever in a film. The way the camera makes the river and locations a part of the story also adds a chilling effect. Even the performances are rather remarkable with all four leads bringing so much personality and development to their characters. I'm really not sure Reynolds was ever better and Voight gives another terrific performance during this hot part of his career, which started a few years earlier with Midnight Cowboy. While the banjo and rape sequences have become the best known I still think the greatest moment in the movie is right after the rape when the men have to discuss what to do. The performances and dialogue really makes this sequence one of the most memorable from the decade, which was full of great movies. I think another reason the movie works so well because it's never really clear what happened to the men. Certain situations come up yet we're never really given the answers as to what is happening and to me this just adds more chills to the movie. But it symbolizes the dualistic relationship of civilization to nature, because music is at once civilized and abandoned. Drew can commune with nature, or communicate with the boy through music, but in the end the boy rejects Drew's desire for friendship. You can be awed by nature's beauty, but to be a part of it requires pain and hardship few are willing to endure.People who are unable to put themselves in a character's place will find this film boring, and will think these men are a bunch of laughable pansies. "},{"Title":"Deliver us from evil.","Date":"6 February 2009","Score":"0 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" Deliverance was a smash hit at the time and still travels well but the film makers made some mistakes that caused the deaths of several people who perhaps tried the stunts in this movie.First, river runners would never take canoes down a river like this. You would take whitewater rafts or kayaks built for the rapids. Running a difficult river takes skill acquired by training, which these characters did not have.Second, river running takes advanced planning and once on the river, scouting the rapids. River runners have to scout the tough rapids ahead of them before they run them. If the rapids are too difficult or unrunnable, the water craft have to be lined down the rapids with ropes.Third, river runners need a shuttle waiting for them at the end of the trip to return them to their vehicles or they need people to shuttle their vehicles to the landing. You must have the utmost trust in the shuttle drivers and pay them well.Watch Deliverance for the drama and suspense; watch The River Wild for white water realism. "},{"Title":"One of the Great Guy Films","Date":"22 January 2003","Score":"0 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":"  Of course the meaning is crystal clear - the difference between civilization - the world of golf, suburban homes and air conditioning - and the wild, where the only law is survival of the fittest, can be paper thin, and these 4 yuppies crossed the line.  The great irony is that these city boys get nailed by the wilderness that their brethren are about to erradicate.  The movie is one of the great icons of the last 30 years - jam packed with chilling moments, funny bits of guy talk, as well as campy scenes and dialogue.  I think the film making is in fact remarkable - the cast was on their own on the river (get the dvd for details); some beautiful scenery was captured as backdrop, and eventually we go along on a Disney ride gone wrong - a hair-raising adventure. This film brings Deliverance mountain men back to screen for the first time since Deliverance in 1972. 33 years after their performance, Maxin Magazine voted them the number one movie villains of all time and AFI has voted Deliverance to be number 15 in all time favorite Thrillers. Ghost Town story was written by Dean Teaster and DJ Perry as an Appalachain tale of folklore character Harmon Teaster from the late 1800's, Dean's great,great, grandfather. It weaves a tale of part of his life with the theme park Ghost Town in the Sky, where Dean's father and Hebert Cowboy Coward acted with the group called The Internationaly Famous Ghost Town Gunfighters. This group of eleven men were RB Coburn's gunfighter crew around 1961 through 1966. During this time they acted along side Hollywood greats such as Clu Gulager, Paul Fix, Frank McGrath, Burt Reynolds, Craig Hill, Donald May, Peter Brown, Veronica Cartwright, Dan Blocker, Tony Dow, John Broomfield, Jon Provost, Ken Weatherwax, Clint walker, Darby Hinton, Grant Sullivan, Larry Matthews, etc. Since Dean's father created the real character of Digger the Undertaker in the theme parks gunfights, he will also play the character Digger in the movie as a tribute to his late father Robert Doyle Teaster. Herbert Coward portrayed Old Zeke and Grandpappy in the gunfights as well, was discovered by Burt Reynolds on his visit to Ghost Town in 1963 when he played Quint Asper for the TV series Gunsmoke. Burt Reynolds later called him for the part of the Toothless Man in Deliverance. Herbert Cowboy Coward lost his two front teeth in a gunfight at Ghost Town in 1962, when Digger was being played by a fill in person in Robert Teaster's absence. The replacement Digger accidentally hit Herbert Cowboy Coward in the mouth with a Colt 45, knocking his front teeth out in front of an estimated 5000 people watching the gunfight. DJ Perry, who wrote this screenplay was able to weave parts of the gunfighter history in with the Harmon Teaster Appalachian's folklore tale. This movie will also be released at the same time of the Theme park re-opening scheduled May 2007, where the Ghost Town Partners (new owners) headed up by Hank Woodburn will return the park back to the Golden Years vision of original builder, Mr. RB Coburn, the mastermind behind Ghost Town in the Sky, Frontierland, and Six Gun Territory in Ocala Florida.Alaska Presley, and her late husband Hugh Presley were instrumental in the opening and operation of Ghost Town in the Sky with RB Coburn, and Russ Pearson, and Uncle Dan Carpenter. Today, she is one of the executive producers of the movie Ghost Town.Robert Bradley, the original character The Apache Kid, from the original crew of gunfighters is also an executive producer of the film.Harry Valentine, the Golden Voice of Ghost Town, is an associate producer of the film and is responsible for making erecting a large polished granite memorial at Ghost Town along with the new owners of the theme park, to created a memorial to the original crew of gunfighters and the Hollywood greats who visited the park in the early years. The memorial will be dedicated to Robert Doyle Teaster, for his portrayal of the character Digger the Undertaker, It will be placed in a waiting lounge area at the foot of the mountain where visitors wait to load the famous mile high chairlift and incline to the top of the mountain at Ghost Mountain Park.The movie will also Starr Stella Parton, Rance Howard, Charlie Mattheau, and a host of others.The movie was originally started by Dean Teaster as a documentary of the original gunfighters and the theme park Ghost Town in the Sky. When DJ Perry was brought on board, the project was turned over to CDI and they teamed up and turned it into this movie. "},{"Title":"One of the finest films ever","Date":"25 April 2006","Score":"2 out of 5 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Adventure, Drama, Thriller","Review":" Deliverance is, in my opinion, one of the finest movies ever made. Several other reviewers have passed comments like 'boring', Obviously subscribers to the brain dead, 2 minute attention span, machine gun edited film school of (much of) modern cinema. Oh dear!Many other reviewers have passed comments like, for the 70's, an excellent film.. Err.., hang on a minute, the 1970s was probably the finest era of Hollywood cinema!! An era when there were; numerous, super talented leading actors who were noted for their ACTING rather than their looks, great directors, and interesting plots - coupled with a coming of age of cinematography and film/camera techniques. The 70's was an era when so many topics (war, love, crime, drama) were made into true classic, gripping must-see movies.Oh HOW I wish the same could be said today!! Getting back to Deliverance! I won't talk about the plot, as other reviews here talk in depth about that. I will simply say that this is one of the afore mentioned 'must-see' movies!! The acting throughout (by the leads and the support - many of whom were genuine locals) is extraordinary. Burt Reynolds in particular blew me away, simply because I never realised (when I first saw it) that he could be so utterly brilliant as an 'straight' actor - rather than a comedic actor. The cinematography is jaw dropping, and the editing is seamless (so unlike today movies today)- slow and relaxed in parts (allowing the film to 'breath') and tight and fast when needed.The sheer beauty of the photography is a joy, and the wonderful scenery and beautiful surroundings belie the horrible situation the four central characters find themselves in.Like all great movies, the movie contains COMPELLING sequences and in Deliverance there are two of note - 1) the dualling banjos scene, and 2) the infamous rape scene. Like the Russian Roulette scene in the Deer Hunter, or the 'Is it safe' dental torture scene in Marathon man - these scenes are utterly gripping, so brilliantly acted and believable, you cannot divert your attention from the screen. Such moments pass into movie legend. Such scenes are full of human drama - a drama that is intensified by the ACTING - and not ruined (as is often the case today) by needless, superfluous fast and rapid editing. The camera is allowed to dwell on actors faces, or actors as a group - so that the interaction is more realistic, and the movie watcher feels more like an 'observer' (instead of just being made to feel dizzy and sick - as is often the case with the over the top editing typical today!) I am 32, and how I envy the cinemagoer of the 70's. Thank god for DVD! The only down side of this movie is the negative image it portrays of the 'South'. I have seen several documentaries of life in the south, and have visited Georgia and the Carolinas. The majority of people I met (and observed on the documentaries) from the region are very friendly and helpful. For many 'southerners', two truths pervade, religion and poverty. Life is very, very tough for many of the people in these regions and they are suitably hardy and phlegmatic about life's struggles and death. There is a gritty and raw side to their character.I have travelled widely throughout the US, and the many of the people of the North Eastern US have not got a clue about life in the South.I don't want to get political, but George Bush Jnr could do with addressing some REAL social concerns (i.e. poverty) in his own country before 'preaching' to the wider world about 'democracy' and 'freedom'.The movie Deliverance portrays the poverty and hardship of the South (that still exists today) accurately, but it gives a disproportionate image of the 'evil' side of the region. Having said that - it is a story!!
